Chapter 2.
It is necessary for us to say in the prologue of this Chapter, summaries of speech and chapters, concerning which understanding is required by the first craftsman at the station of their truth: namely that you, and those who are prompt, know or learn this noble art, and others from us.
I say, therefore, that when it happens to someone a fracture or dislocation or twist or accident: then one must hurry in the first place to bleed him and to resolve him himself, or both at once: unless something prevents, such as weakness of strength: or he is one to whom something happens from those boys, or old men decrepit: or it is a time of very great heat, or very great cold.
Then he should be content with his food, consisting of cool herbs, and meat of birds and lambs: and prohibit wine, and coarse meat, and filling up with food and all food that fills the veins with blood. Until when he is secure from the abscess hot, and does not fear the outflow of matter to the member: then the sick person should return to his first diet with which he was accustomed to his custom.
Therefore when he begins to restore his broken jaw, then the sick person should be fed with nutritious food, much, coarse, heavy, in which there is viscosity: such as made from cooked wheat with water, and rice, and heads, and feet, and bellies of animals, and eggs, and fresh fish: and thick wine, and similar to them. For this diet makes the healing of the broken jaw faster and better.
And you should know that when bones are broken in men who are in their first old age, and the decrepit, it is not possible for them to be continued and consolidated according to their natural way always, because of the dryness of their members: and they are consolidated and continued what is from them in the end of lenity, as the bones of small children.
However nature connects something similar to glue on the broken jaw from every part, in which there is thickness, so that it sticks and binds it, until they are continued together, and tied together, until it comes to the end of strength and firmness, as it was in the first place, until something does not prohibit it from its operations: and for this reason it is necessary, that you put food of the sick person, in which there is heaviness, and viscosity, and thickness, as we said.
And know that the appearance of fractures varies according to the diversity of the limbs, for a fracture of the thigh is different from a fracture of the skull, and a fracture of the ribs is different from a fracture of the spine, and similarly all other limbs differ one from another, and I come with remembrance of all kinds of fractures exposed in their respective chapters distinct from one another. The appearance of fractures also varies within themselves.
For it is broken in an arc shape, besides what happens to it in the stars. And sometimes it is broken along the length of the bone, and its fractures are like stars and additions proceeding, as they are in a reed when it is fitted with a knife for writing. And not proceeding, and there is a fracture with a wound and disruption at the head, and there is a small fracture.
And there is a proper skill or instrument for every kind of fracture, according to which the remembrance of it will come, distinct in its places.
And from the fact that a fracture of a bone is known, there is its curvature and its prominence, and its appearance to the senses, and its sound when you grasp it with your hand.
When therefore there is no curvature appearing in its place, nor sound when you touch the bone with agitation, and you do not find a great weak pain, then it is not a fracture, but possible that there is a curvature or small fracture. Therefore, you should not move it with your hand and compress it at all, indeed put on it from medicines of which the remembrance comes after the moment that suits its place: then it is bound tightly with a thin bandage.
And know that when the wound is incised, it is divided into two parts, besides which certain signs may occur in it; however, all part of it should be removed: then you must begin to correct it from your own hour, equalizing it before it becomes hot and inflamed.
But if an inflammation occurs in it, then leave it for several days until the inflammation subsides. Then equalize it according to any possible manner from lenity and skill.
And know that the restoration of it and its equalization is easier than bone, in which certain signs may occur.
Then bind it, according to what will come to your memory of it.
But if the signs are in bone, then extension of this broken member from two parts is necessary, if the hand is, or the foot: or with your hand, if the member is small: or with two ropes, or with a rope and your hand.
And your position should be where you place the member on an equal place, according to its natural shape, until when the parts of the broken member are extended: then try to make those additions with all skill, and in any manner you can in their places: and beware according to your strength, that pain or injury does not occur from your operation, and try according to your strength, to apply one of two bones with its joint, according to better shapes.
And at that hour, you must try and touch it with your hand. But if you see something different there, correct it, and equalize it by the quantity of your skill.
And beware of violent extension and strong compression, as many fools do. For many often occur as a result of their operation, an abscess causing heat, or contraction in a limb: as we have already seen from their operations many times.
Then after the equation, and a proper operation, and the contraction of that limb, and quietness, and tranquility: and advise the sick one over it. And make him take care, not to move himself at his own watchful hours, and during his sleep, and his awakening, and his agitation, and his excretion, and all his movements with his last power. And that he may take care, not to be in a position of the limb which follows pain. Which is, because when he feels pain in the position of the limb, or injury: then it is necessary to change him to a different position from that with which he does not feel pain.
And take care with him, that his position be that one equal, straight: so that no curvature may occur in the limb when restored.
And the quality of the contraction of the broken limb is, as I tell.
Know that limbs broken vary in their size, and their magnitude, and their form.
Which therefore from them are small, such as the arm, and the finger, and the phalanx, and similar to them, then it is necessary to be a covering of soft cloth, fine. Which however from them are large, such as the hip, and the leg, then it is necessary to be a covering of wide, hard.
Because the broad ligament connects the large limb, and binds it from all parts of the contraction equal, so that it does not enter it spot.
Then after your evacuation, begin to place the suitable liniment on it, in soft wool on the place of the fracture itself. Then begin to wrap the ligament over the place of the fracture with three or four folds, according to what the member requires: and press your hand slightly against the ligament.
Then go with him to the upper part from the place of the fracture, and press less than the pressure you used to press the broken place. Then stretch yourself with the fold from the place of the fracture slightly, and soften the pressure gradually until you take a large piece from the healthy place.
Then take another clean piece of cloth and wrap it again over the place of the fracture with folds. Afterwards go with the fold to the lower part from the fracture. And your operation should be in the pressure of the fold and its softening, as we said in the first fold above.
Then place between the folds a soft wool or cloth with which the crookedness of the fracture will be equal if there is crookedness in it: and if not, you will not put anything on it.
Then wrap another clean piece of cloth over it. Afterwards balance the well-made small stars on top of the folds immediately, if there is no inflation in the place, nor hot abscess.
But if there is inflation or abscess in it, then place on top of it what sits that abscess and removes the inflation: and let it be left for several days. Then press the stars on top of it then.
And the stars should be made from the middle of a wide cane, smoothed, wisely formed: or from the wood from which cribbas are made, that is made from pine, or gerard, that is, branches of palm, or calingi: and the size of all the stars should be according to this form, and this same figure.
However, it is necessary that the nipple placed on the incision should be slightly larger and wider than the other nipples, and its length should be according to the size and strength of the limb.
Then tie another thread on top of the nipple, according to the first tightness. Then tie it with threads from above, as we mentioned about the tightness, and it is that your tightness should be longer over the fracture site, and the farther it is from the fracture, the looser the tightness.
And it is necessary that the threads should be of medium thickness and softness, and made of soft linen.
For if the threads are thick, as I saw from the operations of foolish people who used twisted linen threads, it is a great mistake, because the tightness falls away from them in quality.
And thin threads do not hold again, because you will not catch with the tightness that which you wish.
And it is not necessary that there should be less than a finger's width between the nipples.
If the weak person is harmed by the ends of the nipples after the tightness in healthy places, then place under them a soft wool or carminative wool until nothing is harmed from it.
But when there is a fracture with a wound, and disruption in the skin occurs, I will come again with a remembrance of it in the chapter according to its singularity.
And you should know that not every broken limb needs to be tied with nipples from the first day. That is, because the limb when it is large, should not have nipples placed on top of it, unless after five days, or seven, or more, according to your safety from the event of hot abscesses.
This is the method of applying plasters that the ancients used for healing fractures and dislocations.
The common method of applying a plaster for healing fractures, suitable for many body types, especially women and children, as it is made from a material that does not burn or freeze.
And it is made from the dust of a millstone, which is the fine powder that clings to the walls of the millstone near the moving millstone. You should make it as it is, except for adding some crumbs and the white of an egg. Then place the mixture on the injured area, not too thick or too thin. Then use this plaster.
Another method of applying a plaster for healing fractures, dislocations, and twists. Recipe the following ingredients and grind them: myrrh, laudanum, acacia, enulae, and mugath. Then add equal parts of water and alethil, and grind and mix everything together with alethil water, or with the white of an egg if the patient's body type is weak and prone to heat. Then use this plaster. This plaster has a good composition and heals broken bones quickly and is suitable for many people.
Another method of applying a plaster that also helps with fractures and twists. Recipe the following ingredients and grind them thoroughly: mugath, laudanum, white aloes, myrrh, acacia, and Armenian powder. Then grind and mix everything together with water or the white of an egg, and administer it.
This method of applying a plaster is used for joining bones that have been separated from their original positions, and it soothes the pain caused by the separation of bones, as well as the cut or fracture. The wool is cut, and immersed in vinegar and heated oil, and then placed on a surface.
However, this plaster does not have the power of restoration, but it is good for sedating hot abscesses and expelling pain appropriately.
The method of the plaster that restores a broken jaw. Take the leaves of a silent fig and the leaves of a wild papyrus, and grind all of them, and make a plaster from both.
The method of the shortened plaster, applied for the restoration of a broken bone: and when you want the resolution of the remaining abscess. Take the root of tall althea, and chamomile, and the flowers of wolfberry, and the flour of herb, one equal part of each: grind all of them, and make it into a plaster with wine, if there is no heated limb.
But if indeed it is heated, then make it into a plaster with water of coriander, or with water, and use it.
Another method of the plaster for stronger resolution than this first one, when an hard abscess occurs during the restoration of a limb. Take the root of tall althea, and the seeds of linseed, and balm, and melilot, and sage, and the flowers of wolfberry, and chamomile, one equal part of each: grind all of them, and make it into a plaster with water of willow, or with sweet water, or with wine. All of that according to the heat of the limb, and the quietness of its warmth.
Hippocrates indeed did not say in his book that it should be placed on the broken limb during its restoration, unless it is a bandage made of clay and oil: nothing else: and he said that it should be a medium between thickness and thinness.
According to Galen, it is advisable to place on a broken limb during its restoration those things in which there is a loss of heat, such as myrrh, aloes, and frankincense, and similar substances from among those we have mentioned.
The method of making a plaster that promotes healing and relieves pain. Recipe for making a plaster using equal parts of lupine and chickpea flour, human hair from the incisors, or bird feathers, and salt: grind them together, sieve them, and make a plaster from it.
However, the amount of plaster that should be used is such that it can remain on the bandage without causing pain or itching to the patient, and without moving the broken bone from its place: if this does not happen, do not remove the bandage for many days. If, however, it happens that he has severe itching or pain, or inflammation: then hurry and dissolve the bandage in the hour, and remove the plaster from him: and take a soft cloth or a marine sponge, wet and warm, and dip it in warm water, and wash that place with it until the itching stops and the pain subsides.
If, however, it happens that he has severe itching or pain, or inflammation: then hurry and dissolve the bandage in the hour, and remove the plaster from him: and take a soft cloth or a marine sponge, wet and warm, and dip it in warm water, and wash that place with it until the itching stops and the pain subsides. Then let the limb rest for one hour. Then place on it a piece of dry wool soaked in vinegar and oil, or rose oil: and bandage it overnight, until you are sure that there is no hot abscess, and the inflammation of the limb subsides, and the pain is removed.
Then repeat this tightly and with a small amount of plaster: and do not bandage it with your first tightness and thin strips until it is healed.
If you see that the abscess, redness, pain, inflammation, and all other symptoms have disappeared completely, and you need plaster and bandage, then repeat it as you did at first, evenly.
If something does not happen to a member within the things we mentioned, then you should not remove it unless after three days, or four, or five, or seven. And when it is released after twenty days, according to what appears to you from the removal of the member, as we said, until when the fracture sticks and the coagulation of flesh approaches over it: also add then in the constriction more than your first constriction: and also add in the thickening of the food for the weak, according to what preceded the remembrance of it.
But if you see the place of the fracture already dried up and thinned out more than it should be: then you know that nourishment is prevented from reaching it. Therefore apply it with warm water at every place where you are removing it, and let it be in all three days, and relieve the constriction a little.
Therefore through this operation nourishment reaches the member, and it heals quickly.
But that which the foolish restorers do from the fracture of the member, when it is not restored in the first place according to what it should be, and is restored according to its twist: is an error from their operation, and great fear: and if it were right, they would have said that in their books, and operated with it.
And I did not see any trace of them at all in that: and it is better that it not be done.
¶ On the making of an accidental wound on the head.
The species of fractures are diverse, and their forms are many: and their causes are distinct.
For from a fracture is that which is made by impact with a knife: and the impact removes the whole bone until it reaches the siphac, which is under the bone, just as it does in wood: and because of this this species of fracture is called Axial.
If the knife cuts into a bone and cuts its surface, but does not penetrate to its deepest part, this is called a "Kalaan" fracture, which means an absolute fracture.
And there are two types of wounds from fractures, either major or minor.
From a fracture, there is a type of injury called "contrition" or "attrition," and its cause is being struck by a stone, falling on a stone, or something similar.
This type of fracture is also "penetrating," which approaches the periosteum, which is under the bone, or is on the surface of the bone: and this wound of these two types of fractures is also either minor or major.
From a fracture, there is also a type of injury that is hidden within the bone in its fineness of hair-like structure: and it is a small fissure, and because of this, this type of fracture is called "Capillary.
From these, there is also a type of fracture that occurs by chance, or by being struck by a stone, and similar things that cause the lamina of the bone to enter its interior, and a depression is formed in that place, as happens to copper pots when they are struck, and part of them enters their interior.
This happens most frequently in the heads of moist bones, such as the heads of children's bones.
All these types of fractures have separate signs of displacement, and I will return with a reminder of all of them in their place.
We recognize all these types of fractures by examining them and inquiring about them, and removing the corrupted flesh that is on them.
The "Capillary" type of fracture is recognized by this, that it is scraped over the bone, and cleaned, and smeared with caustic on top of it, for the fracture appears black.
In the treatment of fractures, first consider the accidents of the infirm person: then, if you see from these accidents that it indicates a clear meaning above fear, such as vomiting of cholera, extension, loss of understanding, loss of voice, syncope, acute fever, and egression of the eyes, and redness of them, and similar things to these from the accidents in many arrangements, certainly not healed: and if you see accidents that do not frighten you, and hope for salvation, then begin his cure.
That is, if he comes to you wounded in the first place when he is wounded: and this is in winter days, then it is necessary for you to strive in the removal of the bone before the fourteenth day according to every arrangement.
And if it is in summer days, then it is necessary for you to hasten to remove the bone before the seventh day, before that which is under the bone from the cloth rots, and those accidents happen that we have mentioned.
That is, if the fracture of the bone has already reached the cloth touching the brain, and it is small, and attracted: then it is necessary for you to cut off the part that is broken, and worn away, according to what I tell you.
And it is, to scrape the head of the wounded one: and to uncover the bone as much as possible for you: and according to the shape of the wound, and what is light on the infirm.
That is, if it happens to you at the detection of the bone bleeding blood, or hot abscess: then place it with what is necessary for it. And it is, to fill the place with soaked cloths in wine, and rose oil: and make the wound rest, until the abscess sets, and you are secure from bleeding blood. Then begin with the treatment of the bone and its removal.
And this is done according to one of two methods.
One method indeed is to cut the bone with a sharp-edged incisor: and this is its form.
Another method is to administer another incisor after the first one, a bit wider: and this is its form.
Then also administer another incisor even wider than the second.
The second method is to have a number of different incisors with you, some of which are wider in certain places, and some of which are shorter in certain places: and they should be sharp at the ends of their teeth, and made of iron or bone: and use ease in striking on the incisor, so that it does not shake the head, and does not hurt it.
If however the bone is strong and hard: then it is necessary to make holes around it before you administer incisors, with drills which are called Terebra not penetrating deeply: and they are not so called unless because they do not pass through the end of the fleshy part to that which is after it: therefore, because the Terebro is a round end above it, which is under the sharp head, similar to the edge, and a small circle prevents sinking and passing through the density of the bone.
And it is fitting for you to take from these drills a number of them, each of which fits the thickness of the bone, until you have all the drills present with you: whose sharp end in length and breadth should be according to the thickness of that skull.
And this is the form of three kinds of drills, or trephines.
The quality of drilling around the broken bone is: to place the drill on the bone, and turn it with your fingers until you know that the bone has been penetrated. Then exchange the broach to another location, and place it between all the holes according to the thickness of the radius, or approximately that.
Then cut with the incisors what is between all the holes from the bone: and make it as thin as possible above that from the fine edge, as we said, until you remove the roots of the bone, or by your hand, or with some other instruments which you prepared for that: such as thin scissors and forceps.
And it is necessary to guard against the pits in every precaution, lest the broach or incisors touch anything from them.
When therefore you are drilling around the bone and it has been separated from the pits, if it is continuous with it, and you have removed it, it is necessary to scrape, and make equal the end of it that remains in the bone with another instrument similar to an incisor. But it must be thinner and more delicate than other incisors.
If there remains anything small from the bones and shavings: then take them with ease, with that which is at hand from your instruments. Then care for the wound after that with liniments, and ointments which we said.
And another method of operation is easy, completely far from fear: which he said Galen, and praised it greatly: and this is his word. It is necessary for you in the first place to begin to uncover a part of the bone from the place where the fracture is more and clearer: until when you have uncovered that part, and placed the end of this lenticular incisor under it, whose this is the form.
And the lenticular part does not cut anything: and the sharp part on both sides of it wandering in its length, as you see: so that the lenticular part is hanging over the pits, with its face facing the sharp part in the bone.
Then strike with a small mallet on one side of the Incisorium until you have cut through the entire bone with ease, as it is rolled back. And be secure from the cloth that nothing accidental may happen to it, which you fear entirely.
And if the physician were not more foolish than men and more ignorant of it, nor were he sleeping, then if anything remained attached to the bone from the cloth in certain places, he would cut it off from him with the extremity of the lens-shaped incisor, and free it from him with ease. For it is freed from him without harm, and without fear.
And if the fracture of the bone does not penetrate to the cloth, and that which is cut off from the bone is its face, and there remains in it roughness and subtle thorns: then it is necessary to scrape away that roughness, and uproot those thorns with fine rasps of which you have prepared a variety of sizes, so that it may be possible for you to administer in every suitable place more and better of them, and more and better of their own kind, according to what leads you to the operation, and the fractured bone, and its shape. And it is necessary for you to administer in your own ratio, by which in the first place you cut the bone, more widely of those rasps. Then use after it with finer ones.
And it is necessary for you to administer in your own ratio, by which in the first place you cut the bone, more widely of those rasps. Then use after it with finer ones. And do not cease to do that according to its continuity, until you reach administering thinner and more subtle of all.
In each of the remaining cracks, wounds, and fine fractures, it is necessary for you to administer care, according to what leads to its correction.
And there is a matter which does not remain hidden on all, in this art, and it has stood above that which we wrote and showed in the case of great fractures.
If, however, the bandage is detected and retained when removing the bone: then it is necessary for you to take a cloth of linen, according to the size of the wound, and dip it in rose oil, and place it on top of the wound opening. Then take another cloth doubled or tripled, and dip it in wine and rose oil, and place it on top of the first cloth: and make it as light as possible on top of it, so that it does not weigh heavily.
Then administer the wide bandage above it: and do not tie it, unless by the amount that holds the wound only: and leave it with this care on one day or two days, until you are sure of no hot abscess. Then dissolve it, and administer the following remedies with which there is excessive dryness: such as lily roots, and herb flour, and frankincense powder, and aristolochia, and similar to them: and it is that you make from these remedies a powder, which will be sprinkled on the wound as dry.
And universally administer all the remedy of its property to cleanse and not to bite. And beware according to your skill, that the wound in your treatment is clean: neither is there in it grease of ointment, nor dirtiness.
And beware according to your skill, that the wound in your treatment is clean: neither is there in it grease of ointment, nor dirtiness. Nor dismiss the virus to accumulate in it at all: for when the virus accumulates on the bandage of the brain, it corrupts it, and rots it, and a great evil occurs after that on the weak one: and already it has happened in the head, when it is cleaned by it, especially when its care is neglected, blackness on its surface.
Therefore consider then, if such accidents happen to the weak one as we have said, you know that it is certainly dangerous.
If the blackness does not occur except from the medicine that is placed upon it and is in the power of that medicine to cause that blackness, then it is necessary for you to take a part of honey, three parts of rose oil, and linen cloth with them. Then place it upon the siphac: afterwards, care for it with the species of healing that come together, until it is healed.
¶ On the Restoration of the Nose when it is Broken.
You know that the nose is not broken except from its two upper parts at once, or one of them, because it is two bones: therefore, since the lower part of it is cartilaginous, it is not broken, and only attrition, twisting, and swelling happen to it.
If one of the two parts of it is broken, then it is necessary for you to insert a small finger into the nostril, and make equal that fracture with your index finger and thumb, until the nose returns to its natural form: and do this with skill and ease. And beware, lest pain occur from your operation upon it.
If the fracture is in the upper part of the nose, and does not reach your finger: then it is necessary for you to make equal the end of the radius, where there is a small swelling.
If indeed the fracture is in two parts, then do the same as that: and hasten with the restoration of it on the first day after the fracture, if it is possible. And if not, then on the seventh or tenth day, at the warm baths of pus,
Then insert into the end of the nose a linen cloth, if the fracture is in one part: or insert two linen cloths if the fracture is in two parts: and let the swelling be of such quantity that it fills the nostril.
Certain ancient healers said that linseed should be infused in butter and changed every day. However, I do not see that it is necessary to infuse linseed in butter, but rather with lime in the form of powdered millstone. Then dismiss the linseed until the bone hardens and the cartilage becomes firm.
And sometimes a goose feather is inserted into the place of linseed in the nose, after it has been covered with a soft cloth: so that it holds the broken nose more firmly and does not prevent the weak one from breathing.
This is not a necessary matter. If you wish, do this; and if you wish, make linseed.
If it happens again to the nose while you are operating on the abscess, apply warm plasters of nasal ointment with lime, or with cotton soaked in rose oil, or with a little diaqualon.
But if the abscess does not occur again while you are operating on the hot swelling, then it is necessary to apply plasters of flour of sissam with powdered turpentine and lime in butter. Then place a soft cloth on it, and do not bandage the nose at all.
But if the bones of the broken nose are crushed or broken into small pieces, then it is necessary to split them and remove them with the instruments that are suitable for them. Then sew the cut, and care for it with what heals and consolidates it from suitable plasters or ointments.
But if a wound occurs between the nose and the bone, then it is necessary to care for it with linseed: and administer a lead tube until it heals.
Concerning the restoration of the lower jaw when it is broken.
When the lower jaw is fractured and the fracture is not accompanied by a wound, observe if the fracture is only deformed and not broken into two pieces, and if it can be treated internally: then the diagnosis is easy.
Therefore, if the fracture is in the right jaw, insert the index finger of your left hand into the weak bone: similarly, if the fracture is in the left jaw, then insert the index finger of your right hand: and push with it the roughness of the fracture from inside to outside with your other hand from the inner part of the bone properly, and with it make it even.
If the fracture of the jaw is already separated into two pieces, then it is necessary to apply extension from both parts, according to straightness: until it is possible to make an evening.
But if it has already happened that the teeth are displaced or broken, then tie them with thread made of gold, silver, or wool. Then place a bandage over the fractured jaw. Then place over it a double layer of cloth, and place on the cloth a large star-shaped piece of skin evenly, in the length of the jaw.
Then tie it from above, according to how the ligature is prepared for you and its application fits, until it is not destroyed: and order the weak one to use it with tranquility and quiet: and place its sucking on soft food.
But if you think that something is already changing the shape in some ways: then hurry, and dissolve it on the third day. Then correct what has been changed from it, and make plasters of it from the flour of the mill, with egg white, or with semolina, after you have removed the bandage: and place a soft cloth over the plaster.
While the adhesive plaster remains attached to it and the bone's position is not altered, let it go, do not tear it off, until it heals: or bind the fracture. For often this fracture is bound up for three weeks.
If however meanwhile an hot abscess occurs, then administer what we said should be often given for its sedatio until that abscess is removed.
And if the fracture is with a wound, consider. Now the fragment is already separated from the bone, or from the splint: then carefully take out that fragment of the splint with it which suits you in its removal from the instruments.
But if however the wound's opening is narrow, then enlarge it with a lancet according to the amount of your need.
Then when you have removed that splint, and there remains none of it, then close the wound's opening, if it is large: and if not, place on it from the plasters which suit it, and cover it, until it heals.
¶ On the restoration of the Clavicle, when it is broken.
The clavicle is indeed most often broken from its anterior part on the side of the arm, and its fracture occurs in two ways.
For indeed it is broken and separated into two, except that some splints happen to be in it: and it is easier for its restoration.
Or thus, that some happen to be in the bone of the splints: and it is more difficult for its restoration.
Or thus, that it is a fracture with a wound.
And the operation in it when the fracture is without a wound, is that you make present two assistants, so that one of them holds the instrument following the broken clavicle, and the other extends the neck towards the side.
Then make the fracture of your fingers equal, until it becomes its form, according to what is necessary: and there should not be any projection or hollowness in it.
If you need more extension, then you should place under the infirm sphere a ball of cloth or wool: and its size should be according to your need: and extend, and raise the hook, and cut the sphere with your hand, until it becomes equal to the fracture, according to what is necessary.
If you cannot pull the end of the hook to the outside: because it reaches deep: then you should bend the infirm person on their shoulder: and place a pillow medium in size under their arm, and press their arm downwards, until the hook is raised that is in the depth upwards: and then correct the fracture, and make it equal with your fingers.
If however you feel splintering from the hook, and it moves: then you should chip over it, and extract the splintered part with ease.
If however it is stuck in the bone, then use your skill in the incision of it with one of the prepared incisors, after you have placed under the hook the instrument that will hold the splinter: and this is an instrument made of wood, or of iron. And this is its form.
It resembles a spiral that does not have a hollowness.
And its width should be according to what you need from the size and shape of the bone. Its length indeed also according to what is possible for you in the operation.
And it should have two ends, as you see: and one end should be wide, and the other narrow.
If, however, the disruption of the wound that you cut when extracting the splinter of bone is wide, and you secure yourself from suppuration by heat: then bring together the two lips of the wound with a suture.
And if the disruption is small, or you fear suppuration by heat: then fill the wound with cloth and cotton according to the amount of the wound's cut.
If, however, suppuration by heat occurs, then pour cloth with rose oil, vinegar, and wine, and place it on top of it.
The bandaging of bone when there is no wound and no cut is, to place on the bone a plaster made from flour of molding with animal glue. And place a soft cloth on top of it. Then place a sphere under its tongue, if you need it. Then take a reed, that is, a stick which the Saracens hold, which is long and large: and its width should be that of one palm or about that. Then place a doubled cloth on top of the plaster. Then take a small board from a thin one, whose width is that of three fingers, and in length similarly. Then wrap it in cloth. Then wrap the cloth with the small board in the place where the reed falls on the fracture and the place. Then place the reed on the fracture as it is rolled, and wrap it around his neck, and under the healthy tongue and under the sick tongue: and reduce it over all parts, and how you see that the bandaging holds the fracture properly: and this is what should not be hidden from you, and all of it, so that the small board is not removed above the broken bone. And for this reason, you should examine the sick person every day. When the ligament is softened and you see the star already moving: then correct it and tighten the ligament.
Then place the sick person's sleep on his back, and place a small pillow under his chin during his sleep, so that his support can be raised from his side. Therefore, the fracture of the furcula is raised by elevation of the arm.
Or bind the arm to his neck: and do not release the ligament if it does not occur in the place of accident from itching, or from abscess, until the twelfth day.
Then renew the plaster if you see his face towards it. And add in the stricture, and let him go until he is restored and coagulated.
And the fracture of the furcula is tightened and strengthened as much as possible in twenty-eight days: and sometimes it happens in some people in less than that.
¶ On the restoration of the fracture of the Spatula.
The spatula rarely breaks, and in the wide place of it: and they are not broken from it except its margins.
When therefore it is broken from that place, and broken in the middle of it, and is not known except by touch: then according to what is the figure of the fracture attempt preparation of it. And reduce it to the natural shape in every way by your skill, which is possible for you.
Then place on the place of dust from the mill with white chalk, and woolen cloth, and put a small pillow on top of doubled cloth. Then place a wide flat dish on top of it according to the quantity of the thin plate, or slightly larger than that.
Indeed, if there is a hollow in any part of the thin plate under the dish, then fill that hollow with soft cotton until the dish descends evenly.
Then tie it up from above with a long rope, with proper tension and strength of the dish, until it is not moved from its place.
And consider the rope every day. Whenever the rope becomes soft, tie it again, and if the dish is moved from its place, let the weak side lie on the healthy side.
The thin plate, however, will be restored in twenty days or in twenty-five days.
When this number is completed, then untie the rope: and you will be in safety. For it is from bones whose union should not be feared nor their dissolution.
But if a bone fragment proceeds and punctures under the skin: then cut above it, and remove it. And do what we said about the fracture of the rump from the suppression of abscesses with warm compresses if something happens from it.
Paragraph: On the Restoration of a Fractured Chest.
The chest sometimes breaks in the middle of it, and this rarely happens. Its extremities are broken and crushed most often due to accidents.
When its middle is broken, which leans to the lower parts, and pain and difficulty in breathing, and cough occur, and perhaps vomit blood, and the hollow in the fractured bone is not hidden from sensation, its restoration is that it should be bent weakly on its back: and place between the thin plates a pillow.
Then press his shoulders and gather the ribs from both sides with your hands: and thin them out in their plane, as much as possible and as it is suitable, until they return to the shape of the bone, according to what is required.
Then place a plaster and bandage on him, and place an asterisk above it from a thin board of willow or cherry wood, and similar ones in lightness, after you have wrapped it in cloth. Then thin out the bandage that is tied over the broken jaw, so that it does not move: and go with the bandage according to its roundness towards the back several times, and tighten it with a proper binding.
Then consider the bandage every hour, and whenever it becomes soft, tighten it: and if necessity provokes its dissolution at a sore, or corrosion that happens in the place, or pain, or abscess: then hasten, and dissolve it, and remove the plaster, and correct what has happened with the mode of its cure.
Then reduce the plaster, if you see it to that face: and attach it to a bandage, until it is healed.
¶ On the restoration of the ribs, when they are broken.
Know that the fracture of the ribs does not occur except in places that follow the back and from the ends of them from the front part. And they do not break except for this reason, because they are cartilaginous. And the knowledge of it is not hidden from the sense with the fingers in examination.
And its restoration is made so that the fracture with the fingers in a possible way, until it is equal to the figure, according to what is required. Then apply plasters and bind a fractured jaw with splints, if necessary.
And if the fracture of the ribs is inclined towards the interior: then it happens that the patient experiences severe pain and puncture, as that puncture which occurs to him who has pleurisy: because the bone presses the bandage, and also to him there occurs difficulty in breathing, and cough, and vomiting of blood: and this is difficult to cure.
And already the ancients have been skilled in this with many ingenuities: and from them are those who say, that you should place foods of the sick which generate inflammation and diarrhea, so that the belly is inflamed, and stretched, and the fracture is moved towards the exterior.
We indeed abhor, that there not be a quick cause to make an abscess hot, if it does not occur. But if it has already occurred, then you add to it.
And some of them said: place it on a wet place, then suck it with strength, and it is similar to reasoning. However, one should be afraid that the wet place attracts excessive fluids to the spot, because of the disposition of weakness.
And some of them said: it is necessary that the spot be covered with wool which has already been submerged in hot oil: and they place pads between the ribs, until it is filled: so that it may be a bandage equal when you roll it according to its roundness.
Then the sick will be cured of pleurisy by food and medicine.
But if the sick person is strongly compressed, which he cannot tolerate, and the bone presses the bandage, and wounds with the puncture, and we fear for the sick: then it is necessary, that you cut above the spot, and remove from the broken rib. Then we will place upon these matters an instrument that preserves the tooth, of which mention was previously made: and we will cut through the bone with ease, and we will cut into it.
Then we will gather together two lips of the wound, if it is large with a suture: and we will care for it with plasters, until it heals.
If however an abscess occurs meanwhile, then hurry, and pour warm cloths in rose oil, and place it over the place: and let the sick person be cared for with what is fitting for an abscess.
Meanwhile also it will be bandaged on the side over which he sleeps, until it heals.
Concerning the restoration of a fracture of the spine in the back and neck.
Indeed the bones of the neck sometimes suffer a fracture, and rarely wear away: and similarly the spine in the back also. When therefore it happens to someone, and you wish to know whether he will be healed or not, then consider. For if you see his hands already softened, and stupefied, and dead: and he cannot move them, nor extend them, nor contract them: and when you contract them with your fingers, and prick them with a needle, he does not feel it, and does not feel pain in them: then know that he is not healed, and in many cases it is mortal.
But if he moves them, and feels contraction in them with your fingers, and pricking: then know that the neck of the bone is saved, and that the sick person will be healed with treatment.
But if he suffers a similar fracture in the spine, and you wish to know whether he will be healed or not, then consider his feet: then if you see that they are softened, and what happens in the hands happens to them: and when he lies on his back, wind and excretion come out without his will: and when he is turned on his stomach, excretion comes out without his will: and when he is turned on his back, and wishes to defecate, but cannot: then know that it is mortal. Therefore, do not labor in his care.
If however something does not happen to him from that, the matter is lighter.
And the treatment of what happens from that is, that you try to seek sedatio
Place on that one several times a day until when the boil is sedated: then place on the location one of the strengthening, drying, and binding plasters: and instruct the sick person to use rest and tranquility. Nor should he sleep on the side with which he found pain, until he is healed.
If however something happens at the attrition of the bone sathaiatum, or anything that is already separated from it: then it is necessary, that you separate yourself from the skin, and remove that. Then gather two wound lips, if it is large, with a suture. Then care for that one with some fleshly, from the ointments, until he is healed.
If however the last bone of the tail is broken, and it is the end: then it is necessary, that you insert your left hand's thumb into the anus, and equal the broken bone with your other hand, as possible, and secondarily in equality.
Then place on it, and remove it: and care for the wound with the healing that preceded, until he is healed.
¶ On the restoration of the ankle.
Chapter 11.
The bones of the ankle rarely break. If however they break, then the fracture of them is not, except comminution in the extremities of them: and they are broken in length, and decline to the interiors: and happens pain in the place, and puncture: and astonishes the leg that breaks.
The restoration of it is indeed that you extend your hand upon him, until you stand over the fracture, as its figure is.
But if the fracture is only in its extremities, then equal that fracture, according to what is prepared for you from equality, until it resembles its natural figure.
But if the fracture is in its length, or already inclines to the interiors: then make the sick one lie on his belly, until the restoration of that fracture is prepared for you. When therefore you have equalized it, place upon it a plaster: then place upon it a splint from wood, or from skin: and bind with a bandage above which you do not fear the change of the fracture, nor the removal of the splint.
