¶ Of the restoration of the fracture of the humerus.
The aid is that which is between the elbow and the head of the spoon.
But if it is broken, then its restoration is according to one of two methods.
One of which is: that you take a flat piece of wood of medium thickness, according to this form, which follows.
And two ligaments are tied at both ends.
Then it is hung from an elevated place, and the weak one sits on a seat. Then he throws his broken arm on a piece of wood, until it reaches the titillus, in which the middle is attached to the curved wood.
Then something heavy is hung over him, so that he extends himself towards the lower parts. Then the doctor balances the fracture with his hands at the same time, until the fracture returns as it should be.
And another way is that the weak one lies down on his shoulder: and hangs his hand from his neck with a ligament. Then command two assistants, that one of them holds that which is above the fracture with his hands: and that the other holds below him, and extends each of them to his part.
And if you want, that the extension be stronger, then tie under the fracture with a ligament, and above it with another ligament: and each of the two assistants extends to his two parts.
If therefore the fracture is near the end of the arm, then it is necessary that one ligament be placed under the titillus, and the other under the fracture towards the elbow.
And similarly if the fracture is near the elbow: then it is necessary that the ligament be placed on that place, and on the elbow itself.
Then balance the fracture with ease without labor, until when the fracture is balanced according to what it should be, and applied with good application: then tie it, if not an abscess of hot water occurs.
But if indeed an abscess of hot water occurs, then let go the tie until the seventh day. And place on top of it succulent wool soaked in vinegar and rose oil, until the abscess calms down: then bind it: and the way to bind it is, such that you place a plaster over the fracture.
Then place a new cloth bandage on top of the plaster. Then attach the arm to the support itself, and place her hand open on her shoulder.
And place a cloth, and bind it on top of the arm support, so that the arm stands in the position of the stars: if he does not forbid you from him anything, nor anything is changed above you from the broken bone.
But if you fear that something is changed above you from the broken bone, then use the stars: and it is, such that you place on top of the fracture itself the star that is wider and stronger than the other stars. And place between every star width of a finger: and let the length of the stars be according to the fracture with the addition of three fingers from every part.
Then bind on top of the stars the binding that I said at the beginning of this chapter.
And it is, such that your binding be over the place of the fracture tighter. And the more the fracture extends, the less the binding.
If therefore you see the position of the stars and the binding, as we said, at the hour when you restore the member immediately, then do: and if you fear the warm abscess, then release the binding and the stars up to the seventh day, as we said.
Then consider the bandage in all three days, so that it does not occur in the place of itching, or inflation, or food is prevented from coming to the member, because of the position of excessive binding. So, we will correct this entire thing, as we said.
If indeed you are of good faith, so that nothing unpleasant happens from it, then do not dissolve the ligature until after three days or more.
And let the sick person sleep on his back, and his hand on his belly; and place under his support a stuffed mat filled evenly with wool.
And consider at every hour of the night or day, so that the broken member's figure is not dissolved, nor the ligature softened. So, correct this entire thing by your virtue.
And place the sick person's food according to the order we prescribed, so that the food is subtle in the first place, until he begins to swallow: then it is necessary to make his food thicker.
For the habit of the support and leg is that it should be swallowed in forty days: therefore it is necessary to dissolve, and administer a bath, and treat with ointments that rectify that.
If the fracture is foul and worn: then do not dissolve from it the ligature and stars until fifty days, and until two months.
¶ On the restoration of the fracture of the Arm.
The arm is composed of two bones, which are called two forearms.
One of which is short: and that is the one that follows the thumb. And the other is large, and that is the one that is placed under the small from the lower part.
Perhaps it breaks only the larger forearm, or the smaller: and perhaps they both break at once.
When therefore the smaller forearm is broken above, then its restoration is easy, and its healing is easier.
When the lower part of the bone is broken, it is a bad fracture, and its healing is difficult.
And it is worse when two bones are broken at once.
Therefore, if the bone that is broken is slightly above the lower part, then it is appropriate for the physician to use a gentle extension with ease until it matches it.
However, if the bone that is broken is large, then it is necessary to use a stronger traction quite strongly.
And it is necessary to place the shape of the hand and its extension on the mattress during healing. And the thumb of the hand should be higher than all the fingers: and the joint below all the fingers, or others, and the weak person sitting squarely on himself, and the mattress in front of him, or directly in front of him, at a height, so that the weak person does not incur labor.
Then the servant should extend the arm from the lower part, or with his hand, or with a bandage. And another servant should extend it from above in the same way.
Then the physician should match the bone until he reduces it to the best possible shape.
If there are splinters in the bone fracture, then try to reduce all the fragments to their place by your strength.
However, if it appears in him that the splinter is separate and pierces the skin, and there is no hope of its healing, then cover it over and remove it according to the method we mentioned in those things that were previously mentioned.
However, if the fracture is with a wound, I have already dealt with that chapter alone, therefore take its healing from there.
However, if an abscess with hot pus occurs at the beginning of your healing, then make a linen cloth with a thin layer of rose oil, and white wax, and it should be between thickness and fineness: and bind the cloth over it with a fine traction, until when the abscess is calmed: then remove the wax, and place a prepared plaster made from flour millstone with albumen on it. Then place a splint, and let the splint that is placed on the fracture itself be slightly wider and stronger.
And know that the number of splints for the arm is six in many arrangements: whether the fracture is in one fossa, or in two fossae at once.
Then place your bandage on the stronger and more powerful part of the fracture, and a little more with the bandage towards the upper part of the fracture, or towards its lower part: or place a softer bandage with the bandage for a while, according to the previous mention of it at the beginning of the chapter.
And let the bandages that are wrapped around the fracture be soft, moist cloths: and not hard, rough. And let the thread that is used to tie it be a linen thread, of medium thickness between thin and thick, as we have said.
And consider the limb and the ligament after days. If something happens that requires correction, such as itching that occurs in the limb: then it is necessary to embrocate the limb with warm water until the itching subsides, and let the limb not be constricted until it is quiet, then repeat the bandage.
If the bandage has already become soft, and the bone has already been removed, and similar things: then correct all of it by your virtue, and consider again.
For if nourishment is prevented from reaching the limb because of the excess of the bandage, then it is necessary to soften it a little, and let it be loosed for several days until the nourishment reaches it, then bandage it again.
But if nothing of the things we said happens to the sick person, then it is not necessary to loosen it, unless after twenty days, or about these.
Then suspend the hand of the sick person around his neck. And let his neck be equal, and guard it by its own strength against jerky movements. And make him sleep on his back.
And know that this fracture is healed in thirty days, or in thirty-two days, and perhaps in eighty days. All of this is according to the arrangements of the humors and the arrangements of strength.
§ On the healing of fractures of the hand.
Know that a fracture of the fingers and the knuckles of the hand rarely occurs. And it does not happen to him, unless there is repeated wear.
When therefore a fracture or wear occurs in the toes: then it is necessary for the sick person to sit squarely: and there should be a seat according to equality. Then place his hand on that extended one.
Then the servant should extend the broken bones, and the physician should equal them until they are joined together in a good connection.
Then it is necessary to place a plaster and bandage, if there does not occur a hot abscess. Then place a splint above according to the amount of space: and you have already wrapped it in linen cloth.
If however the fracture is towards the lower part around the belly of the foot, then make a similar sphere from the cloth: and order the sick person to cover it with his broken foot: then it is bound with a long cloth.
And the splint should be from the hide, in which there is softness, so that the hide may be applied to the body of the foot: and bind it, according to what is necessary.
If the fracture is to the outside, then it is necessary to place a splint above and another below in the plantar region, and let the hand be open and straight. Then apply a bandage as the hand is bent, and extend between the fingers with a ligature.
If a fracture occurs in one of the knuckles of the fingers, then if it is the thumb, it will be adjusted according to what is appropriate. Then it will be bandaged with the plantar region: and if it is a deep wound, place a straight splint, small: so that the fracture is elevated and not moved.
But if the fracture is in the other fingers, such as the middle, and index, and auricular, and annular, then it will be adjusted and bound with the healthy finger that follows it: or all will be bound according to continuity: and this is better. Or place a small splint on top of it, as we said in the thumb.
And consider in your restoration, and after the arrangement of abscesses with hot compresses: and place it with what is appropriate, when something occurs from it, according to how its story repeats.
¶ On the restoration of a fracture of the hip.
The hip bone often breaks, and is felt, because it turns to the inner and posterior part.
And its restoration is made so that the ligament is bound above the fracture, and another ligament under the fracture. And the sick person is stretched out on their face.
Then stretch out all the bandage assistant to their part according to equality: and this, when there is a fracture in the middle of the bone.
If, however, the fracture is near the root of the hip, then the ligament should be tied to the part of the patella, so that the extension falls upwards: and another ligament should be tied under the fracture.
And in a similar manner, if the fracture is near the knee, then the ligament near the knee should be tied, so that the extension falls downwards.
Then the physician should equalize the bone with both of his hands, until he reduces it back to its natural shape: and he should join the bone with a good union.
Then one should apply a plaster and bandage, unless it does not happen to the member aphtha. But if it happens to the member aphtha: then let him leave it for several days, until the abscess becomes warm, then return to your treatment.
The bandage of it should be thus. It is necessary to cover over the fracture above with a hard leather, that is, a bandage: and double it twice, or thrice: and there should remain some excess from it. Then cover the leg, until it reaches the calcaneus at the root of the buttocks.
And insert a long rope between the hip and the leg near the knee to the lower parts, and let the ends of the rope come from the upper part of the two parts. Then roll up the excess on the leg and hip from the leather.
Then place above the hip in the place of the fracture some stars, and place one star on the knee joint.
Then fill the spaces between the leg and hip with soft cloths, so that the bandage may be equal.
Then begin with a strong bandage from the middle of the place of the fracture with three turns, or four. And the more you lengthen with the ligament from the place of the fracture, your constriction will be less and softer, and gentler.
Then bend it towards the two ends of the thread, which you have inserted between the hip and thigh. Therefore tie with both of the three stars that are above. Then go with both ends of the thread downwards, until you reach the heel of the foot. And with both also tie the ends of the stars from the other side: so that the ligament will not be removed from its place.
Then let go of the constriction above that, while it remains, so that no corrosion, nor abscess, nor inflation, and similar things to them, will occur in the member. If something happens from that. Then quickly with the solution of it, and correct that which happened from it all, according to what we said many times.
But if in the bone there is a piece from the bone which pricks: then it is necessary, if you can, to make it equal. And if not, find above it, and remove it: and take care with the one of which its remembrance preceded, until it is healed.
And sometimes this fracture of the hip is bound, besides that stars are added to it, as we said in the aid and arm.
However, in the restoration of such a fracture, the weak does not experience limping. And if it is restored alone, besides that the other leg is applied to it, then it is necessary that he who owns it will always limp.
And know that the hip is bound in fifty days, or a little is added to it, or a little is subtracted from it: all of this is according to the diversity of the humors, and the other arrangements.
Translation: On the restoration of a broken kneecap.
Know that the kneecap rarely breaks, but it often becomes worn out.
If indeed it does break, then only a fissure or a hole will be found in its parts. And this is with a wound, and without a wound. And it is treated with the whole body, with sensation.
And its restoration is thus, that it will be made equal to what was broken from its parts with fingers, until it is gathered together and joined together as much as possible according to the equality and facility, and the making.
Then place a plaster made from semolina or milled grain with egg white on top of it: and place a round button on top of it if you need it: and bind it above with a bandage suitable for that.
Then consider all the positions that we have mentioned for other fractures: as boil hot, and similar to it: so, that you place everything with an accident that is suitable for it, until it heals.
Translation: On the restoration of a broken leg.
Know that the leg bone is two bones, of which one is thick and is called the leg bone: and the other is fine and is called the shinbone.
And it happens to both of these from the species of fracture that it happens to two bones of the arm: and because of this, its restoration is made, like the restoration of the arm, equally, and the operation is one.
If both bones are broken at once, the leg is turned to all parts.
And if the fine bone is broken, it turns the leg to the front part.
And if the bone is broken roughly, you will find it below: and there are among them what do not hide from you.
Then apply the extension, equality, and ligation of the stars according to that equally.
However, if the fracture of the leg is a foul fracture, having many splinters, then the extension should be less and gentler according to your relief.
And in the leg itself is something from the operation on the arm: and it is that when you equalize the stars and complete all your operation, then take two bands made of pinewood, which is used in the construction of sun dials that are placed under the tables: and they are made from the fingers of the palm: and choose from them what has a small thickness, and not from the small ones: and let their length be according to the length of the leg from the knee downwards.
Then roll it up on each cloth with two folds according to its length. And place one part of the leg on one side and the other on the other side: and let them be from the knee down to the foot.
Then tie two bands in three places from both ends and the middle. For when stricture is prohibited, the leg leans to the right and left, and stands in a good position.
And indeed, the cradle will be applied according to the length of the leg, and placed in it, the leg, so that it may keep it from movement.
And indeed, you must do this very much in a fracture when with it is a proper wound.
Then consider the leg every day, and study with it with the last effort to avoid suppuration, or swelling, or other things. If therefore something happens from those, then apply to it with what is necessary until it heals, if God wills.
Translation: On the Fracture of Bones in the Foot and Toes.
Fractures of the heel do not occur completely.
Fractures of the bones of the foot and toes do occur sometimes, and they also occur infrequently: and they do not occur to them except from wear in many positions.
If a fracture occurs to the bones of the foot and you see those bones elevated above each other, then place your weak foot on prepared ground, as if walking.
Then stand up and place your foot on that which is elevated from those bones: then press, or step on it: and make it even, until it returns to its places.
Then place a plaster and bandage on top: and place under the sole of the foot a small board, which has two flat heads.
Then bind it with the foot with a proper binding, after you have rolled it up with cloths and other things that are necessary.
Therefore when three days or four pass by him, then loosen the bandage. For you will find the bones evened, whether they are fractured, or dislocated. Therefore the operation in them is as you see.
But if some fingers are broken, then restore them, and make them even as I have told you in the restoration of the fingers of the hand. Then place a splint on your finger according to the length of the finger, and let it be a little wider than that: then place under the foot this board which I have told you, and bind it with a proper binding.
But if that which is broken is from the bones is two, or three, or more, then place on all the finger of its quantity, rolled up in linen: and bind the board in the lower foot: and let it stand on one head of the board outside the sole of the foot, so as to hold well with retention.
And it is fitting for you that you not forget, but rather consider all things that I have told you about the other fractures from the accidents that we mentioned: and oppose each accident with what is appropriate.
On the fracture of the vulva of a woman, and the bone of the beak, and the virgin of a man.
When the vulva of a woman is fractured, then make her sit in a square position. Then lean her towards the back part of her own body, and hold her from behind.
Then the midwife should fill her vulva with cotton, until she fills it: and let it be made into a sphere from that.
Then shake the woman, and elevate her back gradually. For that cotton comes out, until it becomes before the vulva like a sphere, and the fractured bone returns.
Then place a compress on her back. When therefore she wants to urinate, remove the cotton with ease, then she urinates: and reduce it back according to the manner in which you introduced it first: then return to her own compress first.
Let her do that for seven days, or about, for the bone itself is restored.
And if you want, take a catheter, and tie it over the hole of the bone with a reed, and introduce the catheter entirely into her vulva. Then blow in the cannula with force, until the bladder is inflated within the vulva, for the fracture returns.
Then it should be filled with cotton, and let her stand for several days, according to what we have told you, until she is healed.
And when the bone of the beak of a man or woman is fractured, then administer in its restoration, and according to its equality that we have told about in the bone of the anchor.
And the straight is not hidden in these fractures that are close to each other, especially for those who have some study or experience.
And understand my book, for the fracture itself indicates to you in many arrangements the straight path in its restoration and its healing: understand therefore.
But in the penis of a man when it is broken, take the neck of a goose and insert the rod into it. Then place a wrapping of cloth over it and wrap it up, and let it be released for three days or thereabouts, and it will heal if God wills.
¶ On the restoration of bone fractures when there is a wound.
He who has experienced the fracture with the wound, especially if it is large, such as the hip bone, or the shoulder blade, or a similar one, so that you can act quickly, and perform a bloodletting immediately: if the conditions for bloodletting are confirmed to you, as we have previously mentioned.
If the wound bleeds, then you should act quickly to remove it, so that you sprinkle powdered charcoal on top of it: if you do not have any other present.
Then begin to restore the fracture in that same day: and do not postpone it, unless an abscess does not occur.
But if indeed an abscess occurs hot, then let the restoration of it be postponed until the ninth day, until the abscess settles. And do not approach it on the third and fourth days at all, for you would be causing it to suffer bad accidents.
If however the fractured bone is prominent above the skin exposed: then you should try to reduce it, and equalize it with your hands with facility and slight extension.
If, however, you have not prepared an extension for it, and its equality is not achieved by your hands: then reduce it with this instrument. And there is an instrument that is made of iron, whose length is a quantity of seven fingers, or eight: and its width is according to the quantity of the wound. And therefore it is necessary for the physician to take from them three, or four, according to the quantity that he needs for that from the treatment in every kind of fracture.
And let it be round: and let there be a small bulkiness on it, so that it does not double up under compression upon it in an hour of the operation: and let it be sharp at its extremities, with a margin on its extremities. And let it be above its bulkiness from the middle of it up to the lower parts.
And this is its shape.
In this operation, it is necessary to place the sharp end of it curved above the prominent extremity of the bone: and push it with it with one force, until when the bone returns and is made equal by some equality.
Therefore try to make the extremities of the fracture equal again.
If its extremities are subtle, and it does not receive the instrument with good reception, then it is necessary, that you cut the extremity of that bone, until the instrument has power over it.
If indeed you cannot reduce them with the instrument that we have mentioned, then cut it with that which is fitting for it from the knives that we have mentioned: or a saw with one saw in whatever possible way is to you.
Then plane it which remains in the bone from roughness and small bones.
When therefore the bone is reduced, and it finds an infirm person after its reduction suffering from a violent pain, then you know that the bone has not returned to its natural place.
Do this then. For you confer a great comfort to the sick, when your restoration is completed which you make of bones.
Then immerse the cloth in astringent wine, black. And truly if it is in summer. And you do not place on the wound a poultice, nor anything in which there is oil: so that it does not cause decay and putrefaction in itself.
Then administer astellas at the hour of your completion of the restoration of bone. And let the detected wound heal: thus, so that you cut with forceps a hole in the folds according to the amount of the wound.
And beware with all caution, so that you do not tie the wound with a fracture. For many times have the foolish physicians done this: and they caused to happen to their sick ones either death, or corrosion, or zuchum, that is, sain. And your binding should be soft, gentle, different from the bindings of the remaining fractures.
But if the wound is evil, or is a great wound, and you fear some bad accidents which we have mentioned, and you find pain in the hanging place: then it is not necessary to place on it astellas. And make for them folds from hard cloth in the place of astellas, and tie it with them.
But when it is after one day, or two days, and you see the wound beginning to generate pus: then take away from above it the cloth which you placed on it with wine. Then administer liniments, and ointments with which from our custom it is to heal wounds: such as tetrapharmacon oil, and similar to it.
And it is fitting for you to loosen the ligament. And consider the wound every day in the morning and evening, until it is healed and becomes healthy.
And it is necessary to prepare the limb in such a way that pus flows down from it to the lower parts with ease.
But if many days pass by and the wound does not heal, nor is the pus removed from it, then you will know that there are small bones underneath. Therefore, it is necessary to examine the wound with a probe. How many of those small bones are separated, remove them, and take them out. And those of them that are not separated, and pierce the limb, and cause pain, try to cut them, and remove them in any way that is possible for you to do so.
But if a wound becomes infected with pus, or ekileten, that is, estiomenus, or any other sign of corruption and putrefaction, then you must apply to each of them the remedy that is appropriate for it from the cure, whose previous description is in its own chapter.
And in the place where it is necessary for my discourse to stand, and to which you should direct your present mind when a bone breaks and protrudes from the limb, such as the thigh bone, and the shoulder blade, and similar ones from large bones, it is that you do not assume its attraction, for often death occurs from it, indeed, let it go, until it putsrefies.
Perhaps it will fall off by itself after twenty days, or thirty.
Then therefore cure the wound, if it seems to you: and otherwise, let it go.
¶ On the cure of Nodation in the stump of a fractured limb.
This suture is often used for healing a fracture in the hoof. Especially, this one which is near the joints, and it becomes deformed from its figure of a member. And perhaps it prevents the limb from performing its natural function.
Therefore, consider if the suture is recent: administer in it medicines which have astringency, such as aloe, and frankincense, and myrrh, and sarsaparilla, and acacia, and similar to them.
Thus, you will take some or all of these: and mix them with astringent wine, or with egg white, or with vinegar: and place them on the suture in cotton, and tie them over it with a good ligature.
And leave the ligature, but do not loosen it for many days. Then loosen it, and repeat another, until the suture is removed.
Or tie over it a leaden strip properly. For lead is the property of removing everything that is feared from the limbs.
But if the suture has already turned into stone, and is tight, and urgently requires removal: then cut off the superfluous part from above it, and shave it with some razors, until it is removed: and care for it, until it heals.
§ On the cure when the limb is restored, and remains after that delicate, not according to its natural condition.
When the fracture of a bone is restored, and the limb remains after that delicate, weak: then it is not made except for many reasons. One of which is, the excess of the solution of the ligament and its ligature, not according to what it should be: or because of the superfluity of the bandaging of the ligaments, until the nourishment is prevented from reaching the limb: or because of the excess of unnecessary embrocation at an inappropriate time: or because of the lack of blood in the body of the weak person, and its debility.
The treatment for this is to feed the sick person and make his body tender, until the blood multiplies within him. And apply a bath. And bring joy and happiness to him.
Then place the mustard plaster on his limb: so that the mustard draws nourishment much to him. And he should frequently apply the ointment with warm water, until the nourishment is cured, and he returns to his natural shape.
On the treatment of broken bones when they are being straightened.
When it happens that a member already restored after its healing is twisted, and the prominence of the bone broken, or the knotting: and it is deformed by this form of the member, nevertheless the member is not prohibited from its natural operation: then it is not necessary to receive the word of him who thinks that the member should be broken again.
And there have been many foolish physicians and restorers who have done this in our land.
And indeed this operation is very reprehensible, leading to death, or great fear, so that indeed it be mortal.
But if the limping and knotting are recent: then it is necessary to be rubbed with warm water, in which have been boiled softening herbs, such as high hemp, and its root, and melilot, and similar ones. And let an ointment be made for him with a softening plaster, such as diachylon made properly.
Or let the mucilage of the root of high hemp be taken, and be beaten with chicken fat, or with sesame oil: and it is to be plastered with it.
Or they take ripe figs, and crush them with clay from a dovecote, and similar substances which are called 'diminishing consolidations' in medicine.
And sometimes the contraction is solved with frequent, old-fashioned friction, which is done with wet hands. And motion of the member is given to all parts at every hour.
But if the twisting has already preceded, and it has become strong, and has turned into a stone, and necessity has been provoked for its cure with iron: then it is necessary to follow above it, and to dissolve the continuity of the bone, and to cut off what superfluously arises from the twisting or the bone with delicate knives: and to give ease in that with solicitude and study.
Of speech in dislocation.
Dislocation is the exit of some joints from their place. Therefore it is prohibited from its motion, and the figure of the member is deformed. And it causes to happen over the weak pains, and violent injuries.
Therefore when it happens to someone, then it is necessary to hasten to its restoration: nor does it delay it at all.
For if it is delayed, the place becomes an abscess, and perhaps with it the reduction of the dislocation.
Therefore it is not necessary, that it be moved, nor stretched in the hour of its abscess. Because often it causes to happen spasms, and painful injuries.
But when it happens, then it is necessary, that you hasten to the bloodletting of the weak. Then it is dismissed, until the abscess rests.
Then the affected body part should be rubbed with warm water and oil. Then it should be returned with ease.
And all the body parts should be treated with that which is mentioned in its place.
Indeed, I have already arranged the chapters of dislocations according to what preceded in fracture, from the upper part of the body to the lower part of it.
¶ On the care of the dislocation of the lower jawbone.
The two lower jaws rarely dislocate.
And the dislocation of both occurs according to one of two ways. Either indeed they are moved from their places by a slight movement, and softened for a short time. Or they are dislocated completely, and until they soften to the chest part, until the saliva of the sick person flows out, and he cannot hold it, nor can he close his mouth, and his tongue is wrapped with speech.
But when the dislocation of it is small, then it returns to many positions by itself with a little bit.
But if the dislocation is complete and whole: then it is necessary that the reduction of it be administered quickly, and not entirely delayed: and it is necessary for the nurse to hold the head of the sick person: and the physician should insert his one finger of his hand into the root of the lower jaw dislocation between his teeth, if the dislocation is on one side. Or he should insert two fingers of his hand at once, if the dislocation is from both sides. And the other fingers of his hand should be outside, which he balances.
And command the sick person to clean his jaws, and to make them ready for eating at all parts. The doctor balances the mandible and pushes the mandible until it returns to its place.
If it is difficult to return it, especially if both mandibles are involved at the same time, then apply heat with hot water and oil until the reduction of both is easy: do not delay the reduction of both at all, as we said.
Therefore, when they return and balance, and the mouth of the sick person is closed and not softened, then place on them cloths of cloth with wax that has been made from wax: and with rose oil.
Then it will be bound with ease with soft ligatures.
And let the patient sleep on his back: and his head should be between two cloths, so that he does not move it to the right and left: nor should he try or be forced to chew anything: rather, give him soft food, until when the pains are removed, and the jaw is bound: then he eats what he wants, and is given it with ease.
He should not labor over the operation of his mouth at eating and drinking, and swallowing, until the jaw is bound, and he is healed.
If it is difficult to return both mandibles when they are dislocated at some time and do not return to their places, then often fevers and frequent diarrhea occur from this: and perhaps the belly of the sick person is loosened, and perhaps he vomits pure bile.
Therefore, when you see this, then you will know that it is a loss, and often that person dies within ten days who experiences this.
On the reduction of the dislocation of the sternum and the extremity of the arm.
The sternum is not dislocated from the inner side, because of its continuity with the chest. And it is sometimes dislocated towards the outside: and this is indicated by sensation.
And its restoration is that the sick person lies on his back, and extends his arms. Then cut the place with your palm firmly, for it will return.
Then place on it a plaster, and bandages, and bind it.
In fact, the extremities which follow the arm, and are connected with it, do not usually become dislocated, except rarely. But if it becomes dislocated on some day: then it must be reduced and adjusted according to what we said, and that which is prepared for you.
Then place on it a plaster, and bandages, and bind it. And command the sick person to stick to tranquility and quietness until he is healed.
And when this same cure reduces the extremity of the arm, even if it is separated from its place.
¶ On the reduction of the dislocation of the arm.
You should know that the arm does not become dislocated, except in three ways.
One of which is, that it becomes dislocated towards the part of the shoulder to the lower parts. The second is, that it becomes dislocated towards the part of the chest. And perhaps it becomes dislocated towards the upper part of the arm: and this happens rarely.
And it does not become dislocated to the backs, because of the spatula. And it does not become dislocated to the fronts, because of the nerves.
And it is most often dislocated and removed towards the lower parts towards the shoulder: especially in those whose flesh is little. Because I go out of them quickly, and enter quickly.
But in those whose flesh is abundant, it is the opposite, that is, he goes out with difficulty and enters with difficulty.
And perhaps it happens to some people that they are hit or fall, and the shoulder becomes an abscess with heat, and it is thought to be because of that, since it has already been dislocated.
Therefore, one must try this until you are certain of its truth, and then proceed to its cure.
And it is known that the dislocation is when it is towards the inferiors against the acromion: and you distinguish between the dislocated shoulder and the healthy shoulder. For you will find among them a manifest difference, and you will find in the head of the shoulder a hollow, and under the acromion at the touch of the head of the shoulder as if it were an egg: and the weak one cannot reduce his hand to his ear, nor move it with all movements.
And similarly, if it is dislocated towards the chest, or upwards: then you will find a clear touch, and it does not hide.
And this dislocation is easily reduced when it is recent, or the boy is weak.
And its reduction is that the assistant raises his hand to the upper parts. Then you place your thumbs under his acromion, and raise the joint with strength upwards to its place: and the assistant raises his hand and extends it upwards. Then he should put it down below, for it returns quickly.
If not, and if the days since it happened are many: then one must bathe the weak one in hot water, and apply an embrocation that softens and soothes, as if they were boiling the roots of althea, and foenugraecum, and melilot in water, and apply.
Then the sick person should lie on their back: and place a sphere and wool under their chin, which should be equal in softness and tightness.
Then the doctor should place their heel on the sphere, and lift up the arm with strength, and pull the hand of the sick person towards the lower parts.
And if you wish, reduce him according to this manner: and it is, that you make present a man who is longer than the sick person, and stand on the side, and insert his arm under the chin of the sick person: and lift up their chin to the upper parts, until the sick person is suspended in the air. And another assistant should pull the hand of the sick person towards the lower parts of their belly. But if the sick person is light: then it is necessary, that he be suspended with something else, which weighs him down: for the dislocation returns immediately.
And he is also reduced according to another manner: and it is, that a long piece of wood be shaped on the ground, whose head is round in figure: like the handle of a mortar: which is not thick, nor fine. Then place it under the sick person after they have placed cloth over his head on the piece of wood: and the sick person should stand according to the length of the wood.
Then stretch his hand towards the lower parts on the other side, and stretch his body from the other side with strength: for the joint returns to its place with speed.
But if his reduction through all that we have said is difficult: then use this care: and it is, that you take a piece of wood whose length is the quantity of two biceps, and its width is the quantity of four fingers, and its thickness is the quantity of two fingers: which has a round head, so that its entrance into the depth of the chin is eased, according to this form.
Then, clean the round head of the needle, so that it does not wound the weak wood.
Then, place the weak needle under the tilt, so that the hand or arm is stretched over the wood until the lower part: and tie the wood on the support and the arm and the end of the hand.
Then, place the arm on the width of the ladder step, and stretch the hand to the lower part, and let go of the remaining body hanging from the other side. For the joint enters immediately.
Therefore, when the entrance to it is completed according to any possible mode: then it is necessary to place under the tilt an equal quantity of woolen ball from the lamb. Then, place the prepared plaster from the flour mill with bdellium and egg white on the entire arm, as it rolls over.
Then, tie under the tilt a decent binding: and roll the bandage over the plaster above: and attach his hand to his neck, and let go, and do not move his hand for seven days.
And it is necessary to place food for the weak one, until the member is strengthened: because it is faster for its healing.
Then, place a larger one after seven days or five, and exercise it with movement. If therefore it is strengthened and not softened, then it is already healed.
But if the joint is often dislocated due to moisture that happens to it, or for another reason: then it is necessary to administer a cautery in it with three holes, according to what was prescribed in the chapter on cauterization.
But if this is all done, and the bandage is dissolved after seven days, and the joint is not strengthened, and the plaster and binding are rolled back over it several times, and it does not strengthen, and it falls, and it cannot be raised to the upper parts: then you know that the nerves of his arm which are near the head of the humerus, have been cut, or stretched, or softened: and you know that the joint will never be in its place.
For dislocations that occur towards the chest and breasts, or towards the back, reduction is achieved with impulse and extension of the hands: until it returns to its original position, and it is treated with the remaining bandaging and care, until it heals.
If, after healing, there is a severe injury in the limb and slowness in its movement, then the patient should frequently use the bathhouse, until that severity is eased, and it returns to its original nature.
§ On the Treatment of Dislocation of the Elbow.
You should know that the joint of the elbow is dislocated with difficulty, and it also returns to its original position with difficulty: and indeed it can be dislocated in all directions, and especially towards the front or the back.
And its dislocation is not hidden from you: for it falls under the sight and touch, in whatever shape it is dislocated. And when you join the dislocated elbow to a healthy one, it is clearly shown to you. For indeed the joint is bent, and it cannot double the arm, nor touch its own shoulder with it.
And it is necessary for you to act quickly and reduce the dislocation immediately, before an inflammation of hot pus occurs in it.
For if an inflammation of hot pus occurs in it, its reduction becomes difficult: and perhaps it will not be healed at all, especially when it is towards the back. For indeed it is worse in all kinds of dislocations, and more intense pain arises from them: and it often dies along with it.
And its restoration when it is from those that can be reduced, is to extend the hand with both hands: and the arm is expanded, and the hands of the physician are above the elbow and below it, and he pushes the joint with his fingers or with the root of his palm, until it returns to its original place.
Or rather, it is a dislocation towards the front: then he should return as follows, so that he doubles his hand with his own hand, until he strikes with the root of his palm his own elbow which is dislocated.
But if the dislocation does not obey reduction, then administer strong, very forceful extension: and it is, so that two assistants extend the arm, and two assistants also hold him, so that he is not moved during extension.
Then the arm should be rolled back to all parts after it has been unrolled over his hand, with a long doubled cloth or broad ligament.
And when the physician touches the joint with his hand, he should anoint it with oil: so that it is from those which help in the lubricity of the joint with facility. Then he should push the joint with strong impulse, until it returns.
And after its reduction, it is necessary that it be placed on it a plaster, in which there is stipticity, and exiccation with whiting: and it is bound with a proper bandage. And the arm should be suspended from the neck of the weak: and let it be released for several days.
Then it should be unbound. But if the joint is strengthened in its place, then unbind the ligament from it, and let it be released.
And if you see the joint not well bound, then repeat the plaster, and the ligament, and let it be released for several days, until it is bound. Then unbind it.
But if a seriousness occurs to him after the reduction of the joint, and the position is in motion: then administer moisture in bathing, and gentle friction, and compression, until it is lined, or place on the joint, that is, the fat of a young ram. Then bind it up and let her go on that day and at night. Then remove it and put him into a bath. When he sweats, then gently rub the joint with moderate friction. Then repeat the application of the ointment once, and twice, and three times with entry into the bath, until it is soothed.
And if you wish, place on her the warm dung of a cow, moistened with hot butter, and wrap it around him.
Do this several times. For she is indeed soothed, and returns to her proper condition.
¶ On the treatment of dislocation of Almahassem.
Almahassem's hand is often dislocated, that is, the joint that is between the arm and the hand.
And its reduction of dislocation is easy. On the contrary, the rest of the joints. However, it is necessary that the speed of the reduction of this dislocation be in the hour in which it occurs, before the wound closes: or an hot abscess occur in it.
And the reduction of the dislocation is such that one places Almahassem's weak hand on a table, and extends the minister's hand: and the physician places his hand on the prominence of the joint, and pushes it, until it returns.
However, it is necessary to consider if the palm of the hand has already receded to the wrist: then one places the weak back of his hand on the table at extension and reduction: and if the dislocation is proceeding to the back of the hand, then the position of his hand is inside on the table, that is, the inner part of his hand is placed on the table, so that the hand of the physician falls on that prominence of the joint.
If he returns immediately, it is good: and if not, then bind it with a plaster containing a styptic for the stoppage of the abscess, and do not repeat this.
For if he does not tolerate it, and you are unable to apply further reduction to him, after the days pass by, nevertheless the union remains according to its curvature: and nothing harms the sick except that the hand becomes soft, and cannot bind anything: then you know that the nerves have been cut or torn.
There is therefore no other skill in it than to bind it with a cauterizing iron near the wound. And perhaps it does not heal anything.
When therefore Albucasis does not return, then apply the plaster which we have mentioned to him. Then he is bound and let go for five days: after that, it is dissolved and let go, and the hand is exercised.
But if his movement is lost, and something heavy occurs to him: then ease it with warm water, and friction several times, until it is eased.
¶ On the Cure of Dislocations of the Fingers.
When the finger is dislocated from any of them, or the finger from the back of the palm, or its belly: then extend the finger, and push the dislocation with your fingers, until it returns.
Then support the head of the finger, and suspend it turned towards the part to which it is dislocated, and let it go for two days.
Then suspend it, and extend it, until it becomes straight on its day.
When therefore it is night, bind it according to the same account: and do not cease to dissolve it in the day: and exercise it with movement: and bind it at night.
Do this for several days until it is tightly bound.
And do the same with it if it is dislocated towards the palm of your hand: and bind it to that part, and perform your first operation on it until it heals.
And do the same with it when it is dislocated towards the other parts.
¶ On the treatment of dislocation of the sacrum in the back.
When it happens to someone that the sacrum in the back or the neck is completely dislocated, or many spines are moved from their places: then there is no cure for it, because death approaches the weak person.
And the sign of this is that the sickness of the weak person passes out without will, and he cannot hold it: and often some of his limbs are softened from it, or both his legs, or both his arms, or one of them: or one spine is moved from its place.
For often it is moved, because often the movement of them happens to four parts.
Those therefore which are moved towards the back, are called Gibbosity.
The cure of this however is, that you consider at that time, if Gibbosity happens from childhood: then there is no cure for it, nor is there any healing at all from it.
In that however which happens by chance, or by blow, or by similar things, the ancients have already spoken of a lengthy method of cure in their talk. Of which very little returns with profit.
I however have already abbreviated from it, of which little excuses it from much of its proximity to my intention: and from my exposure which I exposed it, and formed an instrument of it in a different way than they showed it, and exposed it.
I say therefore, that in the hardness which occurs from the front part, there is no skill, nor healing from it.
And similarly in that which is made on both sides also.
And it is not treated from them, except that which occurs on the back properly, when I say: and it is, that the weak one be stretched out on his face on a board near the wall: and the wet cloth is spread under him, so that he is not harmed by his chest.
Then a straight piece of wood is placed on the floor, in the end of the board, near his head: and another piece of wood near his feet in the other end of the board: and the servant holds the wood, and it is not firmly fixed in the fork. And the other servant holds the other.
Then he is rolled over on the weak one's head, and under his tongue a strong bandage, firm: and extend the end of the bandage to the wood that is by his head: and tie it in it.
Then tie another bandage above his heels, and above his knees, and by his calves. Then gather all the bandages, and tie them on the other wood that is by his feet.
Then all the servants stretch out to the part of the wood where the bandage is tied, and the two woods are not moved from the places where they are fixed: but they are not tightened, as we said.
And the doctor puts his hands on the spine with strength, until he returns: or puts a board on it. Then he connects his feet on the board, until he returns.
But if he does not return by this cure, then take a board whose length is about three arms. And also prepare a location on the wall that we mentioned is near the weak one, where the end of the board can enter. Then place the middle of the board on the Spondyle. And let the doctor put his feet on the other end. And he should tie it well until the Spondyle is covered, and then return to his place.
Then if you wish, operate with the saw that is wrapped around your hand: and it is to make two pieces of wood on the ground near the head of the sick person in the last of the feet, whose length is the arm of one: and at his head in the last of the feet, two pieces of wood.
And its length should be the same as every wood, a palm: and now there is a hole in every place where the saw ran: and all the wood is firmly fixed on the ground, so that it does not move at all.
And the round wood is inserted: and there is a saw in which the ligament is rolled up in two holes of two pieces of wood.
And at its end is a hole, in which the wood that is firmly fixed, whose length is the same as that which rolls up, is in two other pieces of wood.
Then tie the ligaments that you tied in the chest of the sick person with the saw that is at his head: and the ligaments that you tied in his legs with the saw that is at his feet.
Then stand by every saw, which turns the hand with the leather, with which the saw is rolled up: and the doctor balances the Spondyle, as we said.
This is the shape of the saw that follows.
Then when the Spondyle returns and the place is balanced, then you should put an evaporative plaster with egg white. Then, a bandage. Afterwards, place a stellate bandage above the plaster, whose width is three fingers, or about that. And its length is the amount that will cover the sacrum and on the healthy spine.
Then it is tied with the bandage we mentioned. And the sick person should use this delicate nourishment until he is healed.
If something remains prominent in the place at the end of healing: then it is necessary to administer medicines that soften and alleviate, with the administration of the tablet we mentioned for a long time. And now it is applied to that lead sheet.
And sometimes a prominence occurs at the last spine of the back: and it is thought to be due to dislocation, and that is the bone that already protrudes. Therefore it is not necessary to make a presumption about him with this medicine: for perhaps he will cause death to happen.
Chapter on the treatment of a dislocated ankle.
You should know that the joint of the ankle and the joint of the arm do not occur except in dislocation alone: nor does it happen to them that what happens to the other joints from slight removal and concavity.
And indeed the joint of the ankle is dislocated according to four parts. That is, because it is dislocated towards the front and the back.
And it is very much dislocated towards the inside: and a little dislocated towards the front and the back.
And the treatment of the dislocation towards the inside is because when you join the leg of the sick person with the sick, it is longer: and his knee is higher than that of the healthy one, and the sick person cannot double his foot near the groin: and the place that follows the groin is swollen with hot abscess, therefore because the head of the hip has already reached there.
And its sign, which occurs when there is a dislocation to the exterior, is that it is opposite in nature to these accidents.
And its sign, which occurs when there is a dislocation to the front, is that it stretches the healthy leg according to its complement: however, it does not double it without pain in the knee: and if it tries to walk, it cannot step forward: and it retains its urine, and it develops an abscess in its groin, and it limps when walking, stepping on its heel.
And its sign, which occurs when there is a dislocation to the back, is that it does not stretch the knee, and it cannot double it before doubling the groin: and its leg is also shorter than the other: and the groin is softened, and it is the hip joint at the place of the other.
The reduction of this type of dislocation is indeed, that you should consider whether the dislocation is old and has already outlived its time in the one who has it. Then if you administer its reduction, and it remains according to its position, then there is no cure at all in it. Therefore, it is not necessary to make a complete assumption of it.
But in the one whose dislocation is recent, and it is of a type of four dislocations: then hasten to this, that you fold the joint, and extend it both to the interior and to the exterior, and move it with your right and left hand: for perhaps it returns, and does not require any other cure.
But if it does not return in this way, then you must prepare a strong assistant, and extend the leg from the lower part: or with your hands, or with a bandage that is tied above the leg on the knee: and another assistant extends his hand above, so that he may insert his hands under the tendons of it. Then he ties with a thin ligature over the root of the thigh bone, and holds the end of the other ligament.
And it should be extended from the front part of the groin to the part of the backbone, or from the back to the part of the back.
And it should be extended with one pull of equal strength, until the weak one is lifted up with his body from the ground, and remains hanging.
For this kind of extension is a common kind among the four kinds.
If therefore the dislocation returns with what we said, it is good; and if not, then it is necessary for every kind of that which I will say about its proper cure.
In the proper reduction of it, however, when the dislocation is to the insides, it is necessary that the weak one should lie down on his sound side. Then let him place the ligament over the root of the thigh bone, in that which is between the hip joint and the part under the groin. Then let him extend the ligament upwards from the part of the groin to the higher part of the body, to the part of the backbone.
Then let another strong assistant take his arms, and reduce the large part of the weak one's hip, and extend it to the outer extension with great force. Now indeed it returns to its place.
And this kind is easier of all kinds of cures by which this limb is reduced.
But if it excuses itself to you, and does not obey you with this kind of cure at all: then it is necessary that two weak feet should be bound together with a strong ligament, and a thin one, over the two heels, and over the two knees; and the length of each should be equal to four fingers from its corresponding part: and the weak leg should be extended more than the other by the length of two fingers.
Then the sick person is suspended over the head of a tree, which is in the house, and its length should be equal to the distance from the ground to the armpit of two men.
Then command a strong young man to protect the hip: and suspend him with the sick person in another place: and push the other young man, protecting the hip with strength, for the joint returns to its place with speed.
However, in its proper reduction, when it is a dislocation to the outside, it is necessary for the sick person to lie on the stool, as we mentioned in one with gibbosity. And the tendon is tied over his leg, the sick person properly, and on his chest.
Then two beams are placed, one at his feet, and the other at his head. Then the additional beam is placed in the middle of the foot, firmly fixed: on which wet cloths are laid, so that it does not hurt the sick person: and the beam should be between his hips, so that it is not pulled downwards during extension.
Then all the servants stretch to their part, and the doctor equalizes the reduction by his hand.
If therefore it obeys the return, it will be good.
And if not, place a board on it, and press it evenly, as we said in gibbosity. However, it is necessary for the sick person to lie on his healthy side.
And when the dislocation is to the front, then it is necessary for the sick person's leg to be stretched quickly: and it is according to this same narrative on the stool: and the doctor places his right hand palm on the sick person's hip. Then he binds him with his other hand, and with that binding place him stretched towards the lower part to the knee.
If the dislocation is to the rear: then it is not necessary for the injured person to be extended downwards and raised up on the ground: rather, it is necessary for them to be placed on a hard surface, just as it is necessary for them to be placed in such a position if their ankle is dislocated outwards, according to what we said about their reclining position on two ankles: and the ligaments are stretched, as we said recently.
And it is necessary to apply compression with a board even on the place from which the joint exits, when the reduction is completed according to what is necessary.
And the sign of its reduction is not hidden from you: and it is that you extend both the injured legs: and when you see them equal, and the injured person contracts their leg and expands it without any excuse: then you know that the limb has already returned, according to what is necessary: then join the two thighs and apply a plaster, and bind with imemati, that is, a wheel: in such a way that the ankle does not move towards any part of the body: and let the injured person remain quiet for three days or four.
Then loosen the ligament, and the plaster, and compare the leg with the other: then if you see them equal in position, then you know that the dislocation is already firm: therefore complete the injured person for walking.
And if you see anything of softening: then return and apply the plasters again, and bind it according to your first binding: and let it go for three days also.
Then loosen it and make it slow in walking on both sides for several days until it is strong again.
On Dislocation of the Knee
The knee is dislocated in three ways. The dislocation is directed towards the externals, internals, and inferiors, namely towards the posteriors.
And it is not dislocated towards the anteriors.
And the sign of its dislocation is that you command the sick person to apply his leg to his hip. But if it does not stick to the hip, then you will know that the knee is dislocated.
And the restoration of all its modes of dislocation is to make the sick person sit straight, so that he can extend his leg as much as possible over it, and his back against his face: and place his foot between your feet.
Then hold the knee with your hands, and straighten them across your fingers above the knee. Then apply with your hand two sides of the knee with force. And another servant extends his foot until the knee returns to its place.
And the sign of its return is that the leg sticks to the hip in ease, not with difficulty.
Then apply plasters to him, and make the leg stick to the hip. Then bind it together with a bandage and a splint for three or four days. Then unbind it.
And the sick person should not use walking for several days until he is healed.
But if he refuses to reduce it to you, then apply strong extension with bandages, of which the previous description is given in the cure of the ankle, until it returns.
On the cure of the dislocation of the heel bone.
The heel bone sometimes moves slightly and sometimes is dislocated according to the complement. And its dislocation is made, either towards the internals, or towards the externals.
The sign of its dislocation is that you see the calcaneum (heel bone) inflated, protruding towards the part to which it is dislocated.
However, its cure and removal are easy to restore: and it is to be stretched gently with the hands, and evened, until it returns.
In its cure however, when it dislocates according to the complement, it is necessary that you make the patient sit upright: and hold it firmly with a strong helper from the posterior part of his back, in the middle of itself.
Then hold you with your right hand from the upper part of his foot, and with your left hand from the lower part of his foot in the place of the heel.
Then pull the foot towards you with your right hand. Then with your left hand in two ways.
Then stretch with your left hand, and push the foot's chest towards the leg without labor.
Do this twice, as we have said.
Then push the foot's chest towards the leg in the third way, and you pull the heel.
If therefore it returns to the first way, or to two ways according to this method, and you see the foot evened, it is good: and if not, then repeat the operation on it: for it returns.
But if it is prohibited to you to reduce it with that which we have said: then make the patient lie down on his back on the ground, and fix a firm board in the ground strongly, so that he falls between his hips: and you have already uncovered over him the cloth, so that he does not hurt the patient.
Then let the helper receive his hip: afterwards let the helper extend his foot, or with his hands, or with a band that ties on the neck of the foot. Then every assistant should stand against the extension of his equal: and a staff should be standing between the two hips of the sick person, holding him so that he is not drawn by his body towards the lower parts during the extension.
Then the physician equalizes the dislocation with his hands, and the other assistant holds the healthy leg towards the lower parts. For dislocation returns quickly.
Therefore when the dislocation returns and you are told of its health of return: then place a plaster and bandage, and bind it firmly with bands, and tie the foot with a band to the leg.
And it is necessary that you beware of the cavities or fear the nerves which are above the heel of the back, so that there is not a strong band over them and they are not harmed.
Then let him go for two days or three. If the band is softened, bind it again: then dissolve it on the third day or fourth day: and prevent the sick person from walking for forty days. Indeed if he tries to walk before that time, there is no safety that the dislocation above him does not dissolve and decay, and does not receive after that cure.
If however a boil hot occurs to him: then it is necessary that the attendant in the sedation of him in which our previous account preceded in another place from the cure and ointment, until it is removed.
§ On Dislocation of Toes of the Foot, and Cure.
It is necessary that what is dislocated be equalized with them by extension a few without labor: and indeed this is not difficult, but easy.
If however the dislocation is in certain bends of the back of the foot: then it is necessary that he make the sick person sit his foot on a level with the earth, or on a board: and standing like walking.
Then you should stand and place your foot upon that which is higher than the others among those joints. Then step on it with your foot with strength until it returns, and see that it is now evened out, and does not appear to be raised in place.
Then place a board under its belly which can take the whole foot, with two heads. Then bind it with a decent binding for three days. Then dissolve it and let it rest from walking for many days, until it is bound and secured from the reduction.
¶ On the kinds of dislocation with a wound or fracture.
When something happens to it and you try to cure it and restore it, death often follows: and because of this, it is not fitting for anyone to presume on its cure, which is such as this, unless he is wise in the art with long practice, merciful, pious, slow, not bold, nor presumptuous; and that he use in the beginning of medical treatments which only stop boils with hot: and let him send away the sick by divine judgment. Unless you see or hope for health from the destruction with the alleviation of illness, and in which something appears to be of hope. Then try its reduction immediately at the beginning of the matter, before boil hot occurs.
If then the member returns, as we wish, then administer the regimen which stops boil: and care for the wound with that which is appropriate to it from drying ointments.
If then the dislocation is with fracture, and they occur in the bones of the separated shoulder, then try to remove them, and make in it what we said in simple illnesses from that which preceded in those places.
And keep your wound clean according to your ability, and guard yourself from the entrance into the path of fear: as my previous warning advised you.
For that is better for you before your Creator, and more enduring to your glory, and more saving for your body among them in your time.
